    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="1aa699dbde314706876d4612415c6d95">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'fffe12e7-ffa1-4c9b-ac69-3f98e201af4b'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Zou/Guoqiang">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Hou/Hongshuai" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a9" data-db-target-for="a9" aria-controls="a9" aria-haspopup="true" id="a9_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Hongshuai Hou<i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-mail_outline"></i></span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a9" aria-labelledby="a9_Ctrl" role="region" id="a9">
                     <p class="author-type mb-1">Corresponding Author</p>
                     <p class="author-name">Hongshuai Hou</p>
                     <ul class="rlist sm-account">
                        <li class="sm-account__item"><a class="sm-account__link" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:hs-hou@csu.edu.cn"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-ic_mail_solid"></i><span>hs-hou@csu.edu.cn</span></a></li>

                     <p>State Key Laboratory of Powder Metallurgy, College of Chemistry and Chemical Engineering, Central South University, Changsha, 410083 P. R. China</p>
                     <p>E-mail: <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:hs-hou@csu.edu.cn"><span>hs-hou@csu.edu.cn</span></a></p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Hou/Hongshuai">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Ji/Xiaobo" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a10" data-db-target-for="a10" aria-controls="a10" aria-haspopup="true" id="a10_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Xiaobo Ji</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a10" aria-labelledby="a10_Ctrl" role="region" id="a10">

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d113549384" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Hard carbon (HC) is widely regarded as the most promising anode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). For improving the sodium storage capacity of HC anode, current research primarily focuses on the high-voltage slope region. Actually, increasing the storage capability in the low-voltage plateau region is more important for enhancing the energy density of full cells. Therefore, in this study, HC anode with rich closed pore structure is designed and constructed with the help of carbon dots (CDs), and it is demonstrated that the presence of closed pore structure can provide more sodium storage sites in the plateau region, resulting in an obvious increase of sodium storage capacity. Moreover, the pore-filling and intercalation mechanism for sodium storage in plateau region is revealed by in situ Raman spectroscopy and ex situ transmission electron microscopy (TEM). It is worth noting that the increase in capacity induced by closed pore-filling is not accompanied by a decrease in initial coulombic efficiency (ICE), due to the fact that the introduction of closed pores does not increase the contact area between electrode and electrolyte. This work presents novel concepts for the structural design of HC and provides valuable insights into the effective utilization of plateau region in SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202308392-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202308392-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>With the rapid development of energy storage system, it is urgent to explore eco-efficient and low-cost electrochemical batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0001_R_d113549292e384" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Sodium has the advantages of abundant resources, uniform distribution, low cost, and similar chemical properties to lithium, thus&nbsp;sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have been considered as an effective alternative to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) in some application areas.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0002_R_d113549292e389" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Angew. Chem.">2</a>, <a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0003_R_d113549292e389" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Rare Met.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, as the radius of sodium ion (0.102&nbsp;nm) is larger than that of lithium-ion (0.076&nbsp;nm),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0004_R_d113549292e394" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - SmartMat">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which severely hinders the insertion of sodium ions into anode materials, making the specific capacity and rate performance of SIBs far from ideal, the research of suitable anode materials is a key issue for SIBs at present.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0005_R_d113549292e400" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Fortunately, amorphous carbon materials, especially hard carbon (HC) have become the most promising anode material for SIBs due to their low voltage plateau, chemical stability, low cost, and ease of preparation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0006_R_d113549292e405" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Small Methods">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>The most typical HC has a microstructure including a synergy of randomly distorted pseudo-graphite and amorphous nanodomains with abundant defects and pores of varying sizes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0007_R_d113549292e412" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Universally, HC stores sodium with two voltage ranges, one exhibits a slope capacity in the high potential range (&gt;0.1&nbsp;V) and the other delivers a plateau capacity at low potential (&lt;0.1&nbsp;V).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0008_R_d113549292e417" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Similar to carbon materials for lithium storage in LIBs, sodium ions are stored by three mechanisms: 1) adsorption, 2) insertion, and 3) pore filling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0006_R_d113549292e422" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Small Methods">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, because of the complex diversity of the HC structure, there is still no consensus on its concrete energy storage mechanism. About the different voltage regions, it is worth mentioning that for the slope and plateau regions, the former is controlled by the surface and has faster kinetics, while the latter is controlled by diffusion, and its reaction kinetics is slow.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0009_R_d113549292e428" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Currently, the research on improving the capacity of HC anode is mainly to promote the capacity of slope region by introducing defects and functional groups, increasing specific surface area, and other modification strategies,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0010_R_d113549292e433" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Chem. Eng. J.">10</a>-<a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0015_R_d113549292e433" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Rare Met.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which are often at the expense of initial coulombic efficiency (ICE), especially ignoring the value of plateau capacity at low potential. In fact, the plateau region emerged as a decisive factor influencing the energy density of SIBs. Focusing only on the capacity in the slope region often led to a decrease in the operating voltage of the full battery, particularly evident under conditions where the contribution of plateau capacity diminished at high current densities.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0016_R_d113549292e439" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, from a long-term viewpoint, attention to the contribution of low-voltage platform capacity was necessary. With regard to the sodium storage mechanism, a growing number of studies<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0007_R_d113549292e444" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater.">7</a>, <a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0017_R_d113549292e444" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Energy Mater.">17</a>, <a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0018_R_d113549292e444" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Energy Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> have proven that the specific charge-storage behavior in the plateau region was determined by the microstructure of the carbon material. Xu et al<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0017_R_d113549292e449" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Energy Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> had revealed in detail the charging and discharging process in HC by electrochemical kinetic analysis and operational measurements, and certificated the adsorption mode of sodium ions in the defective parts of the slope region, and further identified the pore filling in the plateau region. Chen et al<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0007_R_d113549292e455" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> established that sodium storage in the platform region was dominated by both carbon layer intercalation and pore filling mechanisms, and the ratio of these two mechanism was controlled through the microstructure of HC. The plateau region behavior of HC containing large amounts of pore was predominantly pore-filled. Therefore, it was evident that although the electrochemical behavior of HC in the plateau region was still controversial, pore construction was the essential way to effectively analyze the plateau voltage mechanisms for improving the plateau capacity.</p>
         
         <p>A variety of works had been published to evaluate the capacity of HC materials through pore formation, but typically, closed pores were introduced via calcination at high temperatures, which was a traditional and single method, and the pyrolysis process at different temperatures had been well understood in recent years.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0017_R_d113549292e463" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Energy Mater.">17</a>-<a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0020_R_d113549292e463" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Energy Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Exploring new methods of pore formation has become a major turning point in the construction of porous HC structures. Carbon dots (CDs), thanks to their ultra-small size and abundant functional groups, had special physicochemical properties and were widely used in electrochemical energy storage applications.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0021_R_d113549292e468" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Phys. Chem. Lett.">21</a>-<a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0030_R_d113549292e468" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - SmartMat">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The complexity of the synthesis process and the low yield of CDs had limited its development on a large scale, but our group reported an efficient and economical synthesis of kilogram-scale CDs by aldol condensation that perfectly avoided these disadvantages.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0031_R_d113549292e473" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - ACS Nano">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> And concretely, the utilization of this type CDs for pore creation was undeniably innovative and valuable, owing to their diminutive size and low polymerization degree, as well as their propensity to deeply infiltrate materials during hydrothermal processes and decomposed at high temperatures, thereby interacting with the interior of carbon materials to generate closed pore structures upon incineration.</p>
         
         <p>This work proposed the utilization of high-yield, cost-effective, and kilogram-scale CDs as an additive to modulate the closed pore structure of HC. During the hydrothermal process for synthesizing polyimide (PI), a series of graded levels of CDs were introduced in situ, resulting in the production of HC materials derived from PI-xCDs composite through high-temperature pyrolysis. Their physicochemical properties, including the degree of graphitization, microscopic morphology, and especially pore structures, had been thoroughly characterized and confirmed to be regular. When applied as the anode in SIBs, their plateau capacity percentages, ICE, <i>D<sub>Na</sub></i><sup>+</sup>, and electrochemical behavior indicated that the introduction of CDs could effectively create closed pores in the HC and enhance diffusion behavior in the platform region. Furthermore, the influence of calcination temperature on both the structure and sodium storage properties of HC was effectively investigated based on the optimal CDs content. The improved outcomes were highly meaningful with an ICE value of 68.42%. Critically, in combination with a series of ex situ and in situ tests, we also established the correlation between the microstructure of materials and their structural evolution during sodium storage process. This study provides an innovative design concept for integrating pore structures into HCs and offers a deeper perspective on the relationship between pore-like structures and sodium storage mechanisms in HCs, both of which may accelerate the development of HC anodes in SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202308392-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202308392-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>Briefly, as depicted in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308392-fig-0001">1</a>, hydrothermal pretreatment of PPA solutions containing varying concentrations of CDs (50-750&nbsp;mg) at 180&nbsp;°C for 10 h was employed to produce PI-xCDs (x was the mass of CDs added), which was subsequently subjected to high-temperature carbonization under Ar atmosphere at 900&nbsp;°C for a duration of 2 h to obtain HCs. The final product with abundant closed pores was denoted as PNDCs-xCDs. Additionally, to thoroughly investigate the pyrolysis process of CDs at elevated temperatures, a series of experiments varying in calcination temperature (800–1300&nbsp;°C) were conducted, and these HC materials were named as PNDCs-500CDs-y°C (y represented the variable of carbonization temperature). The detailed synthesis procedure was outlined in the experimental section.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202308392-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ad11585d-4458-4027-b524-5664a892621f/adfm202308392-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/ad11585d-4458-4027-b524-5664a892621f/adfm202308392-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/14fcbbd7-772f-4e61-bd13-461401b54519/adfm202308392-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ad11585d-4458-4027-b524-5664a892621f/adfm202308392-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202308392-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202308392" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic of the material synthesis for PNDCs-500CDs-1200&nbsp;°C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The morphology and microstructure of the prepared samples were characterized with the help of scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM). First, both the hydrothermally pretreated precursors and the carbon produced by subsequent carbonization exhibited a flaky microscopic morphology, as illustrated in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>–<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information). When the quantity of added CDs reached a certain threshold, both the precursor and final product exhibited significantly enhanced regularity and smoothness in their sheet morphology. Most notably, TEM images specifically focused on several samples, including PNDCs, PNDCs-500CDs, and PNDCs-500CDs-1200&nbsp;°C (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308392-fig-0002">2a–f</a>), revealing a vortex layer structure composed of locally distorted graphite-like domains and micro-pores. However, in contrast to the blank PNDCs, both PNDCs-500CDs and PNDCs-500CDs-1200&nbsp;°C exhibited a prominent number of pore structures upon moderate addition of CDs. TEM pictures of other HC samples containing varying levels of doped CDs (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information) could provide more compelling evidence that the presence of pore structure was positively correlated with CDs concentration. And these pore structures could offer supplementary sites for sodium storage. Further analysis of the HRTEM images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308392-fig-0002">2g,h</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6a–c</a>, Supporting Information) showed that the incorporation of CDs notably increased the layer spacing (d<sub>002</sub>). Specifically, PNDCs-500CDs exhibited an extended d<sub>002</sub> spacing value of 0.392&nbsp;nm compared to 0.375&nbsp;nm observed for PNDCs. But it was worth noting that the excessive addition of CDs should be avoided because it could lead to agglomeration and hinder the expansion of layer distance, as exemplified by PNDCs-750CDs (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6c</a>, Supporting Information). The phenomenon above was also supported by the X-ray diffraction (XRD) spectra presented in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308392-fig-0003">3c</a>. In addition, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308392-fig-0002">2h,i</a> and Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6d–f</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information) clearly demonstrated a decrease in interlayer spacing with increasing carbonization temperature. And as the carbonization temperature got higher, it underwent a marked evolution from a disordered to an ordered structure due to the rearrangement of the carbon atoms, with more graphite-like microcrystalline streaks appearing as defects were reduced.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202308392-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/006c106c-aa21-4550-bb0e-c60af4c763e9/adfm202308392-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/006c106c-aa21-4550-bb0e-c60af4c763e9/adfm202308392-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/156e229f-e209-44d4-a11b-3b301ef87300/adfm202308392-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/006c106c-aa21-4550-bb0e-c60af4c763e9/adfm202308392-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202308392-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202308392" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">TEM and HRTEM of carbon materials: a,d,g) PNDCs; b,e,h) PNDCs-500CDs; c,f,i) PNDCs-500CDs-1200&nbsp;°C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202308392-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/818e85af-3722-403f-9a10-fe875edf23c2/adfm202308392-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/818e85af-3722-403f-9a10-fe875edf23c2/adfm202308392-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5e713a21-99cf-4fb4-8ee6-ecfed604a81e/adfm202308392-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/818e85af-3722-403f-9a10-fe875edf23c2/adfm202308392-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202308392-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202308392" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Raman spectra of samples with a) CDs content and b) annealing temperature as variable. c) XRD patterns and d) pore size distribution of carbon materials with different CDs contents. e) SAXS patterns and f) XPS spectra of PNDCs, PNDCs-500CDs, and PNDCs-500CDs-1200&nbsp;°C. High-resolution N 1s spectra of g) PNDCs-500CDs and h) PNDCs-500CDs-1200&nbsp;°C. i) The high-resolution C 1s spectra of PNDCs-500CDs-1200&nbsp;°C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The information on the structural evolution of these samples had also been accessed through a number of physical structure characterization techniques. The Raman spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308392-fig-0003">3a,b</a>) revealed a gradual increase in the area ratio of the D peak (1350 cm<sup>−1</sup>) to G peak (1580 cm<sup>−1</sup>) with increasing CDs content. This implied that the in situ addition of CDs had led to a higher incidence of defects within the carbon structure during the heat-treating process. Moreover, the aforementioned ratio exhibited a decline as the calcination temperature increased (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308392-fig-0003">3b</a>), signifying a reduction in defects and an augmentation in graphitization, which aligned with the inclination of carbon structure to become more ordered, as evidenced by their HRTEM images. In particular, comprehensive analyses of the pore structure were conducted as illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308392-fig-0003">3d,e</a>. Initially, the porosity of micropores noticeably tended to increase with rising CDs content (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308392-fig-0003">3d</a>). This phenomenon can be attributed to the ultra-small size of CDs on the carbon material's surface, with these open micropores (&lt;2&nbsp;nm) interconnected to the external environment. Furthermore, with a low amount of CDs additive, ultramicropores (&lt;1&nbsp;nm) were mainly formed. Nevertheless, it was worth mentioning that excessively small pore sizes could hinder sodium filling due to the approximate size of Na clusters being ≈1.3–1.5&nbsp;nm.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0009_R_d113549292e626" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> So the amount of CDs added needs to be appropriately large in order to regulate the appropriate pore size. However, a notable inconsistency emerged in the TEM images obtained from the previous study (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308392-fig-0002">2e</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). These images unmistakably depicted a significant presence of mesopores, surprisingly, which were not detected in the corresponding pore size distribution. This incongruity strongly suggested that these mesopores were closed pores, which were impervious to nitrogen gas during the nitrogen adsorption/desorption test. As depicted in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (Supporting Information), elevating the temperature led to a significant reduction in open micropores. This outcome can be attributed to the impact of high calcination temperature, which triggered a series of transformations. Notably, the loss of functional groups occurred, accompanied by the growth of graphene layers and the conversion of open pores into closed pores. Consequently, these changes resulted in a diminished specific surface area and a decrease in defect density. As mentioned earlier, the nitrogen adsorption and desorption test were utilized to analyze the open pore size of the carbon material, while small angle X-ray scattering (SAXS) technique was employed for exploring both closed and open pores as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308392-fig-0003">3e</a>. The presence of nanopores was indicated by the emergence of peaks at Q = 0.1-1 Å<sup>−1</sup> for the three materials under comparison.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0032_R_d113549292e646" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Mater. Chem. A">32</a>, <a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0033_R_d113549292e646" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">33</a>, <a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0009_R_d113549292e646" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In particular, a remarkably prominent peak was observed for PNDCs-500CDs-1200&nbsp;°C, indicating the presence of the largest closed pore diameter among the samples. These noteworthy findings conclusively demonstrated that the closed pore number and diameter of PNDCs-500CDs-1200&nbsp;°C were optimized by carefully incorporating the optimal content of CDs added and precisely controlling the carbonization temperature.</p>
         
         <p>The surface chemical composition was determined via X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS). The XPS spectra in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308392-fig-0003">3f</a> depicted that the carbon materials were comprised of C, O, and N. As the temperature increased, the nitrogen functional group decomposed, resulting in a gradual reduction in nitrogen content as seen from Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9b</a> (Supporting Information), which also had a substantial impact on rate properties of the carbon material. In the high-resolution N 1s spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308392-fig-0003">3g,h</a>), four species of N, pyridinic nitrogen, pyrrolic nitrogen, graphitic nitrogen, and pyridinic N<sup>+</sup>-O<sup>−</sup> appeared correspondingly at the binding energy positions of 398.2, 399.7, 401.7, and 403.2&nbsp;eV, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0012_R_d113549292e669" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Energy Chem.">12</a>, <a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0031_R_d113549292e669" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - ACS Nano">31</a>, <a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0034_R_d113549292e669" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Electrochim. Acta">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The presence of pyridinic N and pyrrolic N provided more defects and active sites for carbon materials, so as to be critical to the kinetic of sodium storage and electrochemical performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0035_R_d113549292e674" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Adv. Energy Mater.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These two compositions were clearly influenced through temperature. But when high temperatures were reached, as depicted in high-resolution N 1s spectrum (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308392-fig-0003">3h</a>), N was apparently dominated by graphitic nitrogen. In the high-resolution C 1s spectrum (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308392-fig-0006">6i</a>), the composition species of PNDCS-500CDs-1200&nbsp;°C were corresponding to C─C/C═C (284.5&nbsp;eV), C−N (285.7&nbsp;eV), C−O (286.9&nbsp;eV), C═O (288.9&nbsp;eV), and O−C═O (290.3&nbsp;eV).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0036_R_d113549292e686" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Energy Storage Mater.">36</a>, <a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0037_R_d113549292e686" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Energy Storage Mater.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Taken together, the parameters presented in <b>Table</b>&nbsp;<a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#adfm202308392-tbl-0001">1</a> clearly indicate that CDs can be effectively utilized as an additive to introduce a closed pore structure into HC materials. Furthermore, based on this fundamental aspect, the physical structure, including the degree of graphitization, proportion of closed pores, and layer spacing, can be finely tuned, and optimized by further adjusting the carbonization temperature.</p>

            <div class="article-section__table-source"></div>
         </div>
         
         <p>The prepared carbon materials were assembled into half-cells to investigate their sodium storage performances. Cycling curves with CDs content and carbonization temperature as variables were demonstrated at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> as revealed in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308392-fig-0004">4b,c</a>. The samples exhibited outstanding capacity retention and cycling performance, with no significant capacity degradation, and the optimal specific capacity was achieved when the content of CDs addition reached 500&nbsp;mg. Importantly, it should be highlighted that the ICE demonstrated a direct correlation with the incremental addition of CDs. It was well known that the value of ICE was predominantly influenced by the formation of solid electrolyte interface (SEI) film and the occurrence of irreversible reactions during initial sodiation process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0038_R_d113549292e880" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - ACS Nano">38</a>-<a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0040_R_d113549292e880" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Eminently, the ICE of PNDCs-750CDs was found to be 63.2%, which was higher than that of PNDCs (54.7%). This trend of ICE aligned with the observed tendency of increased closed pores. The presence of these specific pores effectively limited the contact with the electrolyte, consequently ensuring a significant enhancement in ICE. For HC prepared with optimal CDs addition, the specific capacity was markedly enhanced with increasing the calcining temperature and the ICE was also gradually rose. The highest ICE value (68.4%) was achieved for PNDCs-500CDs-1200&nbsp;°C with optimal variable settings, which can be attributed to the reduced defects and further transition from open to closed pores induced through high calcining temperatures. For the purpose of investigating deeply the influence of the pore structure on the sodium storage capacity of HC, the initial charge-discharge curves of three representative samples (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308392-fig-0004">4d</a>), namely PNDCs, PNDCs-500CDs and PNDCs-500CDs-1200&nbsp;°C, were compared. It was observed that at the same calcining temperature, the charge/discharge curves of PNDCs and PNDCs-500CDs matched closely in the slope voltage region but different significantly in the plateau voltage range. This pointed to the fact that the effect of the introduction of CDs on the sodium storage process primarily occurred at the low potential plateau, leading to the reasonable assumption that the created closed pores can act as sodium storage container in the plateau voltage region. Correspondingly, the alteration of physical structure in carbon material due to elevated calcining temperature not only affected the ICE, but also led to a reduced capacity within the high potential slope range for PNDCs-500CDs-1200&nbsp;°C. The rate performance displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10a</a> (Supporting Information) also indicated that the capacity of these samples with varying amounts of CDs did not notably differ at high current density, and all returned to their initial values during subsequent incipiently small current density. From their charging and discharging curves at different current densities (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308392-fig-0004">4e</a>), it was quite obvious that the high current density had the greatest effect on the capacity of the plateau region. This was because the pore filling corresponded to a slow diffusion behavior where the reaction took place inside the electrode, whereas when the current density was high, the reaction time was too fast to occur deeply inside the electrode, resulting in an almost similar rate performance of these samples.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0041_R_d113549292e895" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Energy Storage Mater.">41</a>, <a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0042_R_d113549292e895" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Turning attention to Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10b</a> (Supporting Information), it became apparent that rate performance exhibited limited efficacy at elevated currents as the calcining temperature increased. In the high-potential slope region, as illustrated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9b</a> (Supporting Information), it was evident that the slope capacity diminished with a reduction in defects and the nitrogen-to-carbon atomic ratio. These defects and surface functional groups could serve as active sites for surface adsorption, contributing to superior kinetics properties. On the other hand, the increased presence of closed pores primarily impacted the plateau region, leading to the suppression of plateau capacity at high current densities and resulting in a decline in rate performance. For a better summary and analysis, according to Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308392-fig-0004">4a</a> which compared the respective ratio of slope and plateau capacity for all samples at the 50th cycle, a clear trend can be observed, whereby the introduction of closed pores promoted an increase in plateau capacity percentage. However, an anomalous situation was observed for PNDCs-50CDs and PNDCs-250CDs, where their plateau capacity did not exhibit a significant variation compared to the blank sample PNDCs. This peculiar behavior can be reasonably interpreted as follows: When adding relatively small amounts of CDs, they might not be sufficient to create a significant number of closed pores, thereby having minimal impact on the plateau capacity. Overall, the best comprehensive properties of PNDCs-500CDs-1200&nbsp;°C were found to be achieved via modulating both the CDs addition and calcining temperature. It was sensible to infer that the construction of closed pores appreciably enhanced the sodium capture ability and pore filling mechanism of HCs in the low potential plateau region.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202308392-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/43ee2b0f-1a0d-4a7b-9b7e-e9bcf14f1682/adfm202308392-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/43ee2b0f-1a0d-4a7b-9b7e-e9bcf14f1682/adfm202308392-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/558ff4b5-f751-4ed9-af55-abbc6af0e4d0/adfm202308392-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/43ee2b0f-1a0d-4a7b-9b7e-e9bcf14f1682/adfm202308392-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202308392-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202308392" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance. a) Specific capacity of all samples from the plateau and slope contribution (discharge capacity at the 50th cycle). Cycling curves of samples with b) CDs content and c) annealing temperature as variable. d) First galvanostatic discharge/charge curves of PNDCs, PNDCs-500CDs, and PNDCs-500CDs-1200&nbsp;°C at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. e) Galvanostatic discharge/charge curves of PNDCs-500CDs-1200°C at different current densities from 0.05 to 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <div class="paragraph-element">To further assess their concrete sodium storage properties, cyclic voltammetry (CV) detection techniques were employed to more clearly distinguish the impact of closed pores on the electrochemical behavior and sodium storage kinetics of the carbon material. The first three CV curves were acquired for PNDCs-500CDs-1200&nbsp;°C at a sweep rate of 0.2&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308392-fig-0005">5a</a>), starting with two irreversible peaks at 0.5 and 1.0&nbsp;V during the first turn of the reduction process, corresponding to the decomposition of the electrolyte and the formation of the SEI film.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0036_R_d113549292e945" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Energy Storage Mater.">36</a>, <a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0043_R_d113549292e945" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Adv. Energy Mater.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> And the large specific surface clearly exacerbated this irreversible reaction. A pair of reversible sharp redox peaks appeared during the next cycle, in response to the desorption and intercalation of sodium ions in the plateau region. At a deeper level, the CV curves were studied at different sweep speeds (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308392-fig-0005">5b,c</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11c</a>, Supporting Information), and the diffusion and pseudocapacitance-controlled behavior can be inferred from this Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202308392-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0044_R_d113549292e960" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202308392-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/67b5683a-f92a-446e-8edd-537421f6d52c/adfm202308392-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202308392:adfm202308392-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202308392-math-0001.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mi>i</mi>
                           <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                           <mi>a</mi>
                           <msup>
                              <mi>v</mi>
                              <mi>b</mi>
                           </msup>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}i = a{v}^b\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div><i>a</i>, <i>b</i> were reaction-related constants, where b values range from 0.5 to 1. When <i>b</i> was close to 1, it denoted an electrochemical behavior dominated by pseudocapacitance, and conversely close to 0.5, diffusivity driven behavior was predominant. On the basis of this, the fitted curves for PNDCs, PNDCs-500CDs, and PNDCs-500CDs-1200&nbsp;°C were shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308392-fig-0005">5e,f</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11d</a> (Supporting Information). The b-values of two peaks were 0.60 and 0.73 in the PNDCs, indicating the presence of both diffusion and surface reactions in its sodium storage process, and the pseudocapacitance reaction with a faster kinetic was specified as the adsorption of Na ions by defects or open pores on the surface.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0045_R_d113549292e992" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Nano-Micro Lett.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, further influence of CDs on the evolution of the closed pore structures can be observed through a set of b values, which were 0.56, 0.69 for PNDCs-500CDs and 0.55, and 0.56 for PNDCs-500CDs-1200&nbsp;°C, respectively. These change revealed that their electrochemical behaviors were almost entirely dominated by diffusion during sodium storage process, and this diffusion was considered to be a sodium ion intercalation in graphite layers as well as nanopore filling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0046_R_d113549292e998" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Adv. Mater.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> On the foundation of the foregoing, the contributions from diffusion-controlled and capacitance-related processes can be rationally estimated from this Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202308392-disp-0002" title="Link to equation">2</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0047_R_d113549292e1006" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Adv. Sci.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202308392-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/c6c8e900-2c6a-4401-b4b8-9024e95ff3d6/adfm202308392-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202308392:adfm202308392-math-0002" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202308392-math-0002.png">
                     <semantics>

         </div>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202308392-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3ef638aa-6594-4236-b994-8af720488533/adfm202308392-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/3ef638aa-6594-4236-b994-8af720488533/adfm202308392-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2129d2d9-f2b9-4ada-8339-36f10a7a50f9/adfm202308392-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3ef638aa-6594-4236-b994-8af720488533/adfm202308392-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202308392-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202308392" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Kinetic analysis. a) CV curves of the first three cycles of PNDCs-500CDs-1200&nbsp;°C at 0.2&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. CV profiles at different scan rates from 0.2 to 1.2&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>, relationship between lg(v) and lg(i), and CV curves displaying the capacitive contribution (pink area) to the total current at 0.6&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> of PNDCs (b, e, and h) and PNDCs-500CDs-1200&nbsp;°C (c,f,i). d) GITT profiles of PNDCs, PNDCs-500CDs, and PNDCs-500CDs-1200&nbsp;°C. g) Capacitive capacity contribution at different scan rates of PNDCs-500CDs-1200&nbsp;°C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>In this equation, <i>k<sub>1</sub>v</i> and <i>k<sub>2</sub>v<sup>1/2</sup></i> were the energy storage contributions from the capacitive and diffusion-controlled process respectively. As depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308392-fig-0005">5g</a>, for PNDCs-500CDs-1200&nbsp;°C, the surface-controlled reaction tended to increase as the sweep speed accelerated and the capacity contribution reached 74.3% at 1.2&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>, thus pointing out that the electrochemical behavior at high sweep speeds was mainly contributed by the pseudocapacitance behavior. Moreover, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308392-fig-0005">5i</a> presented a typical CV curve for PNDCs-500CDs-1200&nbsp;°C at 0.6&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>, illustrating that the contribution of the pseudocapacitance behavior (pink area) to the total electrochemical reaction was 66.3%, while Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308392-fig-0005">5h</a> showed a 73.2% contribution in the PNDCs at the same sweep speed. Furthermore, the difference in energy storage process behaved clearly in the plateau region, as can be clearly observed according to the comparison of the detailed area distribution of the two materials. Hence the introduction of closed pores would mainly play a role in low potential region to enhance diffusion response, which further confirmed the contribution of pore filling in the plateau.</p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">With a view to gaining insight into the dynamics of the electrode process, the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT technique) was used to approximate the diffusion coefficient of Na<sup>+</sup> as described in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308392-fig-0005">5d</a>. This procedure utilized a pulsed current of 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup> for 20 min, accompanied by a 2.0&nbsp;h rest interval. The diffusion coefficient was calculated according to the following Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202308392-disp-0003" title="Link to equation">3</a>):

            <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202308392-disp-0003"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/74d8c088-8f40-4230-b7fb-c32396876266/adfm202308392-math-0003.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202308392:adfm202308392-math-0003" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202308392-math-0003.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mi>D</mi>
                           <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                           <mfrac>
                              <mn>4</mn>
                              <mrow>
                                 <mi>π</mi>
                                 <mi>τ</mi>
                              </mrow>
                           </mfrac>
                           <msup>
                              <mrow>
                                 <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
                                 <mfrac>
                                    <mrow>
                                       <msub>
                                          <mi>m</mi>
                                          <mi>B</mi>
                                       </msub>
                                       <msub>
                                          <mi>V</mi>
                                          <mi>M</mi>
                                       </msub>
                                    </mrow>
                                    <mrow>
                                       <msub>
                                          <mi>M</mi>
                                          <mi>B</mi>
                                       </msub>
                                       <mi>S</mi>
                                    </mrow>
                                 </mfrac>
                                 <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
                              </mrow>
                              <mn>2</mn>
                           </msup>
                           <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                              <mfrac>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi>
                                    <msub>
                                       <mi>E</mi>
                                       <mi>s</mi>
                                    </msub>
                                 </mrow>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi>
                                    <msub>
                                       <mi>E</mi>
                                       <mi>t</mi>
                                    </msub>
                                 </mrow>
                              </mfrac>
                           </mfenced>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}D = \frac{4}{{\pi \tau }}{(\frac{{{m}_B{V}_M}}{{{M}_BS}})}^2\left( {\frac{{\Delta {E}_s}}{{\Delta {E}_t}}} \right)\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>where <i>τ</i> (s), <i>m<sub>B</sub></i> (g), <i>V<sub>M</sub></i> (cm<sup>3</sup> mol<sup>−1</sup>), <i>M</i><sub>B</sub> (g mol<sup>−1</sup>) and S (m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>) were the pulse time, the active mass of the electrode, the molar volume, the molecular weight, and the active surface area of the electrode, respectively. In addition, ∆<i>E</i><sub>S</sub> and ∆<i>E</i><sub>τ</sub> were derived from the GITT curves. As depicted in the Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308392-fig-0005">5d</a>, while the diffusion coefficients of three materials exhibited similar magnitudes, there were notable variations in their distribution across the voltage range of 0.01–3&nbsp;V. Once the voltage fell below 0.1&nbsp;V, the diffusion coefficient values of PNDCs-500CDs and PNDCs-500CDs-1200&nbsp;°C exhibited a sharp decline in diffusion coefficient values. This validating that when the voltage decreased from sloping to plateau region, the electrochemical reaction transformed from surfaced-controlled to diffusion-controlled. Moreover, the diffusion coefficient values of PNDCs remained relatively stable during this potential interval, implying that a distinct energy storage mechanism existed between the PNDCs and CDs-regulated carbon material in this region. Clearly, this Na-storage model can be summarized that the sloping region corresponded to the faster adsorption of sodium ions in the superficially defects or open pores, and the plateau region conversely referred to the lower diffusion of sodium ions at closed pores.</div>
         
         <p>In an effort to reveal the sodium storage mechanism of this carbon material in more detail, a series of ex situ and in situ characterization techniques were employed to research energy storage process. After the initial discharge, ex situ TEM was used to investigate the change of microstructure of PNDCs-500CDs-1200&nbsp;°C, which suggested that the quantity of pores was significantly reduced in comparison to its original state (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308392-fig-0006">6a</a>). This phenomenon was attributed to the pore-filling of sodium ions during discharge process, as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308392-fig-0006">6b</a>. To further verified the existence of this mechanism from multiple perspectives and examine the reversibility of the pore filling process, the microstructure of the electrode after ten charge/discharge cycles was investigated, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308392-fig-0006">6c</a>. It can be seen that the pore structure had recovered well after several charge/discharge cycles, further proving the existence of the pore filling mechanism and good reversibility of this behavior. Furthermore, the same electrodes as above had also been analyzed via HRTEM technique, as described in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308392-fig-0006">6d–f</a>. It was found that the layer spacing was expanded, from 0.39 to 0.449&nbsp;nm after sodiation process, which was caused by the intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> into carbon interlayer. And after ten cycles of charging and discharging, the layer spacing returned to 0.384&nbsp;nm, again demonstrating the excellent reversibility for sodium storage. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308392-fig-0006">6g</a> showed how sodium ions were stored in the HC, corresponding to “adsorption-pore filling/intercalation” mechanism.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202308392-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c5516e2d-9a1c-4dfb-9a9d-2f644c1d0a15/adfm202308392-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/c5516e2d-9a1c-4dfb-9a9d-2f644c1d0a15/adfm202308392-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2f90883c-b25c-44da-92ac-04298f0b8c23/adfm202308392-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c5516e2d-9a1c-4dfb-9a9d-2f644c1d0a15/adfm202308392-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202308392-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202308392" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">TEM, and HRTEM of PNDCs-500CDs-1200&nbsp;°C at the states: a,d) pristine; b,e) the first discharged state; c,f) the 10th cycled states. g) Simple schematic description of the sodium storage mechanism in porous carbon materials.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Additionally, in order to explore the contribution of closed pore to the plateau in a more in-depth manner, in situ Raman spectroscopy was used to monitor the structural change during the sodiation process of the HC. The Raman spectra of PNDCs-500CDs-1200&nbsp;°C had typical D and G peaks (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308392-fig-0003">3b</a>). The disordered band (D band) was associated with the A<sub>1g</sub> breathing mode of the sp<sup>2</sup> carbon atom in the ring and its presence was caused by a structural defect. In contrast, the graphite band (G band) corresponded to the E<sub>2g</sub> in-plane bond-stretching motion for all pairs of sp<sup>2</sup> carbon atoms in rings and chains, reflecting the degree of graphitization of HC.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0048_R_d113549292e1250" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308392-fig-0007">7a,b</a>, selecting PNDCs-500CDs-1200&nbsp;°C as a representative sample which was rich in closed pores. In the slope voltage region (&gt;0.1&nbsp;V), the D peak and <i>I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub></i> decreased with the extent of discharge process, indicating that the sodium ion interacted with the defect site at the surface of electrode and suppressed the A<sub>1g</sub> mode of D band.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0049_R_d113549292e1270" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Energy Environ. Sci.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, there was no significant shift in the position of the G peak during this process, which suggested that Na<sup>+</sup> did not react with the sp<sup>2</sup> carbon atoms in this region.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0017_R_d113549292e1279" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Energy Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Together with the predominantly fast kinetic behavior of this step as analyzed through various electrochemical techniques, it can be concluded that the sodium storage mechanism at this stage corresponded to surface pores or defects adsorption. During the plateau voltage range (&lt;0.1&nbsp;V), the D-peak broadened and gradually disappeared, representing that the interaction between sodium ions and defects or pores still existed.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0007_R_d113549292e1285" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, it was worth noting that G-peak position was turned to the left with its width increased, indicating that C─C bond changed by charge transfer from sodium atoms to <i>π</i><sup>*</sup> antibonding orbitals of carbon atoms. In other words, this manifested that sodium ions diffuse to suitable closed pores and interact with sp<sup>2</sup> carbon atoms, thus affecting the E<sub>2g</sub> stretching mode of the G-peak.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0017_R_d113549292e1298" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Energy Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> All these phenomena strongly revealed the pore filling mechanism in closed pores during this low potential region. Additionally, the G-peak clearly shift to a lower wavenumber, suggesting Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation between the stacked graphene layers.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308392-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308392-bib-0007_R_d113549292e1305" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Combining this in situ Raman result with previous kinetic analysis, it was found that the sodium storage in the HC is dominated by the adsorption of Na<sup>+</sup> in active sites/open pores in the slope region and the pore filling/insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> into closed pores/carbon interlayer in the plateau region.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202308392-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f24c9b83-fea8-4292-8b74-6999d179627f/adfm202308392-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/f24c9b83-fea8-4292-8b74-6999d179627f/adfm202308392-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/70b9d38b-ad59-442a-8c5a-bdf389943046/adfm202308392-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f24c9b83-fea8-4292-8b74-6999d179627f/adfm202308392-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202308392-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202308392" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">In situ Raman spectra of a,b) PNDCs-500CDs-1200&nbsp;°C and e,f) PNDCs-1200&nbsp;°C; c) Schematic illustrations of the sodium storage mechanism in HC; d) Simple schematic diagram of in situ Raman device.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>What's more, in order to make comparisons for highlighting the influence of the closed pores structure for sodium storage, the variable of temperature was excluded. Similarly, in situ Raman characterization was performed for PNDCs-1200&nbsp;°C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308392-fig-0007">7e,f</a>). First, in the slope region, it can be found that its <i>I</i><sub>D</sub><i>/I</i><sub>G</sub> decreased notably, which corresponded to the adsorption at defect or open pores. Meanwhile, this slope capacity accounted for up to 85% as demonstrated by the galvanostatic discharge curve, namely the adsorption mechanism dominated a large proportion of the overall sodium storage process. While observing the plateau region again, it can be revealed that the relative intensity of D-peak reduced less compared to PNDCs-500CDs-1200&nbsp;°C, indicating that the pore filling does not play an impressive contribution in this stage. Prominently, the shift of G peak to the left indicated that the alteration of the microstructure with closed pore did not affect the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> in the graphite layer. And this insertion mechanism was dominated in the low potential plateau region of PNDCs-1200&nbsp;°C. Therefore, the comparison above pointed to an “adsorption-pore filling/intercalation” mechanism for sodium storage in HC (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308392-fig-0007">7c</a>), but the details were determined by the microstructure, and the simple introduction of closed pores can greatly raise the contribution of the pore filling mechanism without affecting the intercalation of sodium ions in the carbon layer, thus increasing the ratio of platform capacity.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202308392-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202308392-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>To enhance the electrochemical performances of hard carbon anode for sodium ion batteries, hard carbon materials with abundant closed pores were designed and prepared. It was found that carbon dots can be utilized as a pore-forming additive to create closed pore structure, which provides a new strategy to construct carbon materials with closed pore structure. It was noteworthy that the pore filling behavior of Na<sup>+</sup> for PNDCs-500CDs-1200&nbsp;°C with abundant closed pores exhibited more pronounced prominence in the low voltage plateau region compared to PNDCs-1200&nbsp;°C without closed pores, demonstrating that the introduction of closed pore structures can significantly provide more sodium storage sites through pore filling mechanism in the plateau voltage region, thereby enhancing sodium storage capacity in the low potential. And it was demonstrated that the sodium storage mechanism of hard carbon is “adsorption-pore filling/intercalation” behavior, which was proved through in situ Raman spectroscopy and ex situ TEM. This study provides a valuable reference for comprehending the sodium storage mechanism of hard carbon and proposes a novel experimental approach to design high-performance HC anode with closed pore structures.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
